	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">International law</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Law of Nations" redirects here. For the 18th-century political treatise, see <a href="/wiki/The_Law_of_Nations" title="The Law of Nations">The Law of Nations</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Regulations governing international relations</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo-de-Groot-Oudaen-Patrick-Le-Clerc-Van-de-waarheid-des-christelyken-godsdiensts_MG_1338.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Hugo-de-Groot-Oudaen-Patrick-Le-Clerc-Van-de-waarheid-des-christelyken-godsdiensts_MG_1338.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Hugo-de-Groot-Oudaen-Patrick-Le-Clerc-Van-de-waarheid-des-christelyken-godsdiensts_MG_1338.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="363" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Hugo-de-Groot-Oudaen-Patrick-Le-Clerc-Van-de-waarheid-des-christelyken-godsdiensts_MG_1338.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Hugo-de-Groot-Oudaen-Patrick-Le-Clerc-Van-de-waarheid-des-christelyken-godsdiensts_MG_1338.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Hugo-de-Groot-Oudaen-Patrick-Le-Clerc-Van-de-waarheid-des-christelyken-godsdiensts_MG_1338.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Hugo-de-Groot-Oudaen-Patrick-Le-Clerc-Van-de-waarheid-des-christelyken-godsdiensts_MG_1338.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3307" data-file-height="5455" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hugo-de-Groot-Oudaen-Patrick-Le-Clerc-Van-de-waarheid-des-christelyken-godsdiensts_MG_1338.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illustrated title page "Hugo the Great of the Truth of the Christian Worship." Along with the earlier work of <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Vitoria" title="Francisco de Vitoria">Francisco de Vitoria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alberico_Gentili" title="Alberico Gentili">Alberico Gentili</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Grotius" title="Hugo Grotius">Hugo Grotius</a> laid the foundations for international law.</div></div></div><p><b>International law</b>, also known as <b>public international law</b> and <b>law of nations</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> is the set of rules, norms, and standards generally  accepted in relations between <a href="/wiki/Nation" title="Nation">nations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-definition_–_international_law_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-definition_–_international_law-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> It establishes normative guidelines and a common conceptual framework to guide states across a broad range of domains, including war, diplomacy, trade, and human rights. International law thus provides a means for states to practice more stable, consistent, and organized international relations.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sources_of_international_law" title="Sources of international law">sources of international law</a> include <a href="/wiki/Customary_international_law" title="Customary international law">international custom</a> (general state practice accepted as law), <a href="/wiki/Treaties" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaties">treaties</a>, and general principles of law recognized by most national legal systems. International law may also be reflected in <a href="/wiki/Comity" title="Comity">international comity</a>, the practices and customs adopted by states to maintain good relations and mutual recognition, such as saluting the flag of a foreign ship or <a href="/wiki/Enforcement_of_foreign_judgments" title="Enforcement of foreign judgments">enforcing a foreign judgment</a>.
</p><p>International law differs from state-based <a href="/wiki/Legal_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal system">legal systems</a> in that it is primarily—though not exclusively—applicable to countries, rather than to individuals, and operates largely through consent, since there is no universally accepted authority to enforce it upon <a href="/wiki/State_Sovereignty" class="mw-redirect" title="State Sovereignty">sovereign states.</a> Consequently, states may choose to not abide by international law, and even to break a treaty.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> However, such violations, particularly of customary international law and peremptory norms (<i><a href="/wiki/Peremptory_norm" title="Peremptory norm">jus cogens</a></i>), can be met with coercive action, ranging from military intervention to diplomatic and economic pressure.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Monism_and_dualism_in_international_law" title="Monism and dualism in international law">relationship and interaction</a> between a national legal system (<a href="/wiki/Municipal_law" title="Municipal law">municipal law</a>) and international law is complex and variable. National law may become international law when treaties permit national jurisdiction to <a href="/wiki/Supranational_union" title="Supranational union">supranational</a> tribunals such as the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Human_Rights" title="European Court of Human Rights">European Court of Human Rights</a> or the <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Court" title="International Criminal Court">International Criminal Court</a>. Treaties such as the <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Conventions" title="Geneva Conventions">Geneva Conventions</a> may require national law to conform to treaty provisions. National laws or constitutions may also provide for the implementation or integration of international legal obligations into domestic law.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">Terminology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The term "international law" is sometimes divided into "public" and "private" international law, particularly by civil law scholars, who seek to follow a Roman tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Roman lawyers would have further distinguished <i><a href="/wiki/Jus_gentium" title="Jus gentium">jus gentium</a></i>, the law of nations, and <i><a href="/wiki/Jus_inter_gentes" title="Jus inter gentes">jus inter gentes</a></i>, agreements between nations. On this view, "public" international law is said to cover relations between nation-states and includes fields such as <a href="/wiki/Treaty_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty law">treaty law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Law_of_sea" class="mw-redirect" title="Law of sea">law of sea</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_criminal_law" title="International criminal law">international criminal law</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Laws_of_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Laws of war">laws of war</a> or <a href="/wiki/International_humanitarian_law" title="International humanitarian law">international humanitarian law</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_human_rights_law" title="International human rights law">international human rights law</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Refugee_law" title="Refugee law">refugee law</a>. By contrast "private" international law, which is more commonly termed "<a href="/wiki/Conflict_of_laws" title="Conflict of laws">conflict of laws</a>", concerns whether courts within countries claim jurisdiction over cases with a foreign element, and which country's law applies.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A more recent concept is "<a href="/wiki/Supranational_law" title="Supranational law">supranational law</a>", which concerns regional agreements where the laws of nation states may be held inapplicable when conflicting with a supranational legal system to which the nation has a <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">treaty</a> obligation.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Systems of supranational law arise when nations explicitly cede their right to make certain judicial decisions to a common tribunal.<sup id="cite_ref-Degan1997_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Degan1997-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The decisions of the common tribunal are directly effective in each party nation, and have priority over decisions taken by national courts.<sup id="cite_ref-Blanpain2010_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blanpain2010-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> is most prominent example of an international treaty organization that implements a supranational legal framework, with the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Justice" title="European Court of Justice">European Court of Justice</a> having supremacy over all member-nation courts in matter of <a href="/wiki/European_Union_law" title="European Union law">European Union law</a>.
</p><p>The term "transnational law" is sometimes used to a body of rules that transcend the nation state.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_international_law" title="History of international law">History of international law</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tratado_de_kadesh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Tratado_de_kadesh.jpg/220px-Tratado_de_kadesh.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="338" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0a/Tratado_de_kadesh.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="260" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tratado_de_kadesh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Hittite version of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian%E2%80%93Hittite_peace_treaty" title="Egyptian–Hittite peace treaty">Treaty of Kadesh</a>, among the earliest extant examples of an international agreement.</div></div></div>
<p>The origins of international law can be traced back to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_history" title="Ancient history">ancient times</a>. Among the earliest examples are peace treaties between the <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamian</a> city-states of <a href="/wiki/Lagash" title="Lagash">Lagash</a> and <a href="/wiki/Umma" title="Umma">Umma</a> (approximately 2100 BCE), and <a href="/wiki/Egyptian%E2%80%93Hittite_peace_treaty" title="Egyptian–Hittite peace treaty">an agreement</a> between the Egyptian pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_II" title="Ramesses II">Ramses II</a> and the Hittite king, <a href="/wiki/%E1%B8%AAattu%C5%A1ili_III" title="Ḫattušili III">Hattusilis III</a>, concluded in 1258 BCE. Interstate pacts and agreements of various kinds were also negotiated and concluded by <a href="/wiki/Polity" title="Polity">polities</a> across the world, from the eastern Mediterranean to East Asia.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a>, which developed basic notions of governance and international relations, contributed to the formation of the international legal system; many of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_ancient_treaties" title="List of ancient treaties">earliest peace treaties</a> on record were concluded among the Greek city-states or with neighboring states. The <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> established an early conceptual framework for international law, <i>jus gentium</i> ("law of nations"), which governed both the status of foreigners living in Rome and relations between foreigners and <a href="/wiki/Roman_citizenship" title="Roman citizenship">Roman citizens</a>. Adopting the Greek concept of <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a>—the idea that certain rights are inherent to all humans—the Romans conceived of <i>jus gentiumas</i> as being universal. However, in contrast to modern international law, the Roman law of nations applied to relations with and between foreign individuals rather than among political units such as states.
</p><p>Beginning with the <a href="/wiki/Spring_and_Autumn_period" title="Spring and Autumn period">Spring and Autumn period</a> of the eighth century BCE, China was divided into numerous <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_Han" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic Han">ethnic Han</a> states that were often at war with each other. Subsequently, there emerged rules for diplomacy and treaty-making, including notions regarding the just grounds for war, the rights of neutral parties, and the consolidation and partition of states; these concepts were sometimes applied to relations with <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_minorities_in_China" title="Ethnic minorities in China">non-Han</a> "barbarians" along China's western periphery.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The subsequent <a href="/wiki/Warring_States_period" title="Warring States period">Warring States period</a> saw the development of two major schools of thought, <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">Legalism</a>, both of which held that the domestic and international legal spheres were closely interlinked, and sought to establish competing normative principles to guide foreign relations. Similarly, the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> was characterised by an ever-changing panoply of states, which over time developed rules of neutrality, treaty law, and international conduct. Embassies both temporary and permanent were established between states to maintain diplomatic relations, and relations were conducted with distant states in Europe and East Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following the <a href="/wiki/Collapse_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Collapse of the Western Roman Empire">collapse of the western Roman Empire</a> in the fifth century CE, Europe fragmented into numerous often-warring states for much of the next five centuries. Political power was dispersed across a range of entities, including the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Church</a>, mercantile city-states, and kingdoms, most of which had overlapping and ever-changing jurisdictions. As in China and India, these divisions prompted the development of rules aimed at providing stable and predictable relations. Early examples include <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon law</a>, which governed <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiology" title="Ecclesiology">ecclesiastical</a> institutions and clergy throughout Europe; the <i><a href="/wiki/Lex_mercatoria" title="Lex mercatoria">lex mercatoria</a></i>  ("merchant law"), which concerned trade and commerce; and various codes of <a href="/wiki/Maritime_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Maritime law">maritime law</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Rolls_of_Ol%C3%A9ron" title="Rolls of Oléron">Rolls of Oléron</a>—which drew from the ancient Roman <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_law#The_Sea_Laws" title="Byzantine law"><i>Lex Rhodia</i></a>—and the <a href="/wiki/Laws_of_Wisbuy" title="Laws of Wisbuy">Laws of Wisby</a> (Visby), enacted among the commercial <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a> of northern Europe and the Baltic region.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Concurrently, in the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic world">Islamic world</a>, foreign relations were guided based on the division of the world into three categories: The <i><a href="/wiki/Divisions_of_the_world_in_Islam" title="Divisions of the world in Islam">dar al-Islam</a></i> (territory of Islam), where Islamic law prevailed; <i>dar al-sulh</i> (territory of treaty), non-Islamic realms that have concluded an armistice with a Muslim government; and <i>dar al-harb</i> (territory of war), non-Islamic lands whose rulers are called upon to accept Islam<i>.</i><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Under the early <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphate</a> of the seventh century C.E., <a href="/wiki/Islamic_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic law">Islamic legal principles</a> concerning <a href="/wiki/Islamic_military_jurisprudence" title="Islamic military jurisprudence">military conduct</a> and the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Prisoners_of_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Prisoners of war">prisoners of war</a> served as precursors to modern <a href="/wiki/International_humanitarian_law" title="International humanitarian law">international humanitarian law</a>. Islamic law in this period institutionalised humanitarian limitations on military conduct, including attempts to limit the severity of war, guidelines for ceasing hostilities, distinguishing between civilians and combatants, preventing unnecessary destruction, and caring for the sick and wounded.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The many requirements on how prisoners of war should be treated included providing shelter, food and clothing, respecting their cultures, and preventing any acts of execution, rape, or revenge. Some of these principles were not codified in <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western</a> international law until modern times.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the European <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, international law was concerned primarily with the purpose and legitimacy of war, seeking to determine what constituted a <a href="/wiki/Just_war_theory" title="Just war theory">"just war</a>". For example, the theory of armistice held the nation that caused unwarranted war could not enjoy the right to obtain or conquer trophies that were legitimate at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The Greco-Roman concept of natural law was combined with religious principles by Jewish philosopher <a href="/wiki/Moses_Maimonides" class="mw-redirect" title="Moses Maimonides">Moses Maimonides</a> (1135–1204) and Christian theologian <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> (1225–1274) to create the new discipline of the "law of nations", which unlike its eponymous Roman predecessor applied natural law to relations between states. In Islam, a similar framework was developed wherein the law of nations was derived, in part, from the principles and rules set forth in treaties with non-Muslims.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emergence_of_modern_international_law">Emergence of modern international law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Emergence of modern international law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The 15th century witnessed a confluence of factors that contributed to an accelerated development of international law into its current framework. The influx of <a href="/wiki/Greek_scholars_in_the_Renaissance" title="Greek scholars in the Renaissance">Greek scholars</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_byzantine_empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Fall of byzantine empire">collapsing Byzantine Empire</a>, along with the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a>, spurred the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">development of science, humanism, and notions of individual rights</a>. Increased <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">navigation and exploration</a> by Europeans challenged scholars to devise a conceptual framework for relations with different peoples and cultures. The formation of centralized states such as Spain and France brought more wealth, ambition, and trade, which in turn required increasingly more sophisticated rules and regulations.
</p><p>The Italian peninsula, divided among various city-states with complex and often fractious relationships, was subsequently an early incubator of international law theory. Jurist and law professor <a href="/wiki/Bartolus_de_Saxoferrato" title="Bartolus de Saxoferrato">Bartolus da Saxoferrato</a> (1313–1357), who was well versed in Roman and Byzantine law, contributed to the increasingly relevant area of "<a href="/wiki/Conflict_of_laws" title="Conflict of laws">conflicts of law</a>", which concerns disputes between private individuals and entities in different sovereign jurisdictions; he is thus considered the founder of <a href="/wiki/Private_international_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Private international law">private international law</a>. Another Italian jurist and law professor, <a href="/wiki/Baldus_de_Ubaldis" title="Baldus de Ubaldis">Baldus de Ubaldis</a> (1327–1400), provided voluminous commentaries and compilations of Roman, ecclesiastical, and <a href="/wiki/Feudal_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudal law">feudal law</a>, thus creating an organised source of law that could be referenced by different nations. The most famous contributor from the region, <a href="/wiki/Alberico_Gentili" title="Alberico Gentili">Alberico Gentili</a> (1552–1608), is considered a founder of international law, authoring one of the earliest works on the subject, <i>De Legationibus Libri Tres</i>, in 1585. He wrote several more books on various issues in international law, notably <i>De jure belli libri tres</i> (<i>Three Books on the Law of War</i>), which provided comprehensive commentary on the laws of war and treaties,
</p><p>Spain, whose <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">global empire</a> spurred <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Golden_Age" title="Spanish Golden Age">a golden age</a> of economic and intellectual development in the 16th and 17th centuries, produced major contributors to international law. <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Vitoria" title="Francisco de Vitoria">Francisco de Vitoria</a> (1486–1546), who was concerned with the treatment of the indigenous peoples by Spain, invoked the law of nations as a basis for their innate dignity and rights, articulating an early version of sovereign equality between peoples. <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Su%C3%A1rez" title="Francisco Suárez">Francisco Suárez</a> (1548–1617) emphasized that international law was founded upon the law of nature.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grotius_-_De_jure_belli_ac_pacis,_1680_-_206.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Grotius_-_De_jure_belli_ac_pacis%2C_1680_-_206.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Grotius_-_De_jure_belli_ac_pacis%2C_1680_-_206.tif.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="336" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Grotius_-_De_jure_belli_ac_pacis%2C_1680_-_206.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Grotius_-_De_jure_belli_ac_pacis%2C_1680_-_206.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Grotius_-_De_jure_belli_ac_pacis%2C_1680_-_206.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Grotius_-_De_jure_belli_ac_pacis%2C_1680_-_206.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1916" data-file-height="2923" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grotius_-_De_jure_belli_ac_pacis,_1680_-_206.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hugo Grotius' <i>De jure belli ac pacis</i>, is considered one of the foundational texts of international law. (Pictured is the title page from the second edition of 1631).</div></div></div>
<p>The Dutch jurist <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Grotius" title="Hugo Grotius">Hugo Grotius</a> (1583–1645) is widely regarded as the most seminal figure in international law, being one of the first scholars to articulate an international order that consists of a "society of states" governed not by <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a> or <a href="/wiki/Warfare" class="mw-redirect" title="Warfare">warfare</a> but by actual laws, mutual agreements, and customs.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Grotius secularized international law and organized it into a comprehensive system; his 1625 work, <i><a href="/wiki/De_jure_belli_ac_pacis" title="De jure belli ac pacis">De Jure Belli ac Pacis</a></i> (<i>On the Law of War and Peace</i>), laid down a system of <a href="/wiki/Sources_of_international_law" title="Sources of international law">principles of natural law</a> that bind all nations regardless of local custom or law. He also emphasized the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_the_seas" title="Freedom of the seas">freedom of the high seas</a>, which was not only relevant to the growing number of European states exploring and colonising the world, but remains a cornerstone of international law today. Although the modern study of international law would not begin until the early 19th century, the 16th century scholars Gentili, Vitoria and Grotius laid the foundations and are widely regarded as the "fathers of international law."<sup id="cite_ref-Jr.20122_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jr.20122-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Grotius inspired two nascent schools of international law, the naturalists and the positivists. In the former camp was German jurist <a href="/wiki/Samuel_von_Pufendorf" title="Samuel von Pufendorf">Samuel von Pufendorf</a> (1632–94), who stressed the supremacy of the law of nature over states. His 1672 work, <i>De iure naturae et gentium,</i> expanded on the theories of Grotius and grounded natural law to <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a> and the secular world, asserting that it regulates only the external acts of states. Pufendorf challenged the <a href="/wiki/Hobbesian" class="mw-redirect" title="Hobbesian">Hobbesian notion</a> that the state of nature was one of war and conflict, arguing that the natural state of the world is actually peaceful but weak and uncertain without adherence to the law of nations. The actions of a state consist of nothing more than the sum of the individuals within that state, thereby requiring the state to apply a fundamental law of reason, which is the basis of natural law. He was among the earliest scholars to expand international law beyond European Christian nations, advocating for its application and recognition among all peoples on the basis of shared humanity.
</p><p>In contrast, positivist writers, such as <a href="/wiki/Richard_Zouch" title="Richard Zouch">Richard Zouche</a> (1590–1661) in England and <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_van_Bynkershoek" title="Cornelius van Bynkershoek">Cornelis van Bynkershoek</a> (1673–1743) in the Netherlands, argued that international law should derive from the actual practice of states rather than Christian or Greco-Roman sources. The study of international law shifted away from its core concern on the law of war and towards the domains such as the law of the sea and commercial treaties. The positivist school made use of the new scientific method and was in that respect consistent with the empiricist and inductive approach to philosophy that was then gaining acceptance in Europe.
</p>
<h4><span id="Establishment_of_.22Westphalian_system.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Establishment_of_&quot;Westphalian_system&quot;">Establishment of "Westphalian system"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Establishment of &quot;Westphalian system&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alberico_Gentili.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Alberico_Gentili.jpg/220px-Alberico_Gentili.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Alberico_Gentili.jpg/330px-Alberico_Gentili.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/30/Alberico_Gentili.jpg 2x" data-file-width="368" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alberico_Gentili.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Alberico_Gentili" title="Alberico Gentili">Sir Alberico Gentili</a> is regarded as the Father of international law.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The developments of the 16th century came to a head at the conclusion of the "<a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Peace of Westphalia</a>" in 1648, which is considered to be the seminal event in international law. The resulting "<a href="/wiki/Westphalian_sovereignty" title="Westphalian sovereignty">Westphalian sovereignty</a>" established the current international legal order characterized by independent sovereign entities known as "<a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation states</a>", which have equality of sovereignty regardless of size and power, defined primarily by the inviolability of borders and non-interference in the domestic affairs of sovereign states. From this period onward, the concept of the sovereign nation-state evolved rapidly, and with it the development of complex relations that required predictable, widely-accepted rules and guidelines. The idea of <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a>, in which people began to see themselves as citizens of a particular group with a distinct national identity, further solidified the concept and formation of nation states.
</p><p>Elements of the naturalist and positivist schools became synthesised, most notably by German philosopher <a href="/wiki/Christian_Wolff_(philosopher)" title="Christian Wolff (philosopher)">Christian Wolff</a> (1679–1754) and Swiss jurist <a href="/wiki/Emer_de_Vattel" title="Emer de Vattel">Emerich de Vattel</a> (1714–67), both of whom sought a middle ground approach in international law. During the 18th century, the positivist tradition gained broader acceptance, although the concept of natural rights remained influential in international politics, particularly through the republican revolutions of the United States and France. Not until the 20th century would natural rights gain further salience in international law.
</p><p>Several legal systems developed in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, including the codified systems of continental European states known as <a href="/wiki/Civil_law_(legal_system)" title="Civil law (legal system)">civil law</a>, and <a href="/wiki/English_common_law" class="mw-redirect" title="English common law">English common law</a>, which is based on decisions by judges and not by written codes. Other areas around the world developed differing legal systems, with the Chinese legal tradition dating back more than four thousand years, although at the end of the 19th century, there was still no written code for civil proceedings in China.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>
Until the mid-19th century, relations between states were dictated mostly by treaties, agreements between states to behave in a certain way, unenforceable except by force, and nonbinding except as matters of honor and faithfulness. One of the first instruments of modern international law was the <a href="/wiki/Lieber_Code" title="Lieber Code">Lieber Code</a> of 1863, which governed the conduct of U.S. forces during the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Civil_War" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Civil War">U.S. Civil War</a>, and is considered to be the first written recitation of the rules and articles of war adhered to by all civilized nations. This led to the first prosecution for war crimes, in which a Confederate commandant was tried and hanged for holding prisoners of war in cruel and depraved conditions at <a href="/wiki/Andersonville_National_Historic_Site" title="Andersonville National Historic Site">Andersonville</a>, Georgia. In the years that followed, other states subscribed to limitations of their conduct, and numerous other treaties and bodies were created to regulate the conduct of states towards one another, including the <a href="/wiki/Permanent_Court_of_Arbitration" title="Permanent Court of Arbitration">Permanent Court of Arbitration</a> in 1899, and the <a href="/wiki/Hague_Conventions_(1899_and_1907)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hague Conventions (1899 and 1907)">Hague</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geneva_Conventions" title="Geneva Conventions">Geneva Conventions</a>, the first of which was passed in 1864.</p><div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg/230px-Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg" decoding="async" width="230" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg/345px-Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg/460px-Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1882" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Original_Geneva_Conventions.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/First_Geneva_Convention" title="First Geneva Convention">First Geneva Convention</a> (1864) is one of the earliest formulations of international law</div></div></div>
<p>The concept of sovereignty was spread throughout the world by European powers, which had established colonies and spheres of influences over virtually every society. Positivism reached its peak in the late 19th century and its influence began to wane following the unprecedented bloodshed of the <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">First World War</a>, which spurred the creation of international organisations such as the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>, founded in 1919 to safeguard peace and security. International law began to incorporate more naturalist notions such as <a href="/wiki/Self-determination" title="Self-determination">self determination</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a>. The <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">Second World War</a> accelerated this development, leading to the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, whose <a href="/wiki/Charter_of_the_United_Nations" title="Charter of the United Nations">Charter</a> enshrined principles such as nonaggression, nonintervention, and collective security. A more robust international legal order followed, which was buttressed by institutions such as the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a>, and by multilateral agreements such as the <a href="/wiki/Genocide_Convention" title="Genocide Convention">Genocide Convention</a>. The <a href="/wiki/International_Law_Commission" title="International Law Commission">International Law Commission</a> (ILC) was established in 1947 to help develop, codify, and strengthen international law
</p><p>Having become geographically international through the colonial expansion of the European powers, international law became truly international in the 1960s and 1970s, when rapid <a href="/wiki/Decolonization" title="Decolonization">decolonisation</a> across the world resulted in the establishment of scores of newly independent states. The varying political and economic interests and needs of these states, along with their diverse cultural backgrounds, infused the hitherto European-dominated principles and practices of international law with new influences. A flurry of institutions, ranging from the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organisation</a> to the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Organization" title="World Trade Organization">World Trade Organisation</a>, furthered the development of a stable, predictable legal order with rules governing virtually every domain. The phenomenon of <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalisation</a>, which has led to the rapid integration of the world in economic, political, and even cultural terms, presents one of the greatest challenges to devising a truly international legal system.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_relations">International relations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: International relations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sources_of_international_law" title="Sources of international law">Sources of international law</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_ICJ_cases" class="mw-redirect" title="List of ICJ cases">List of ICJ cases</a></div>
<p>Sources of international law have been influenced by a range of political and legal theories. During the 20th century, it was recognized by legal <a href="/wiki/Legal_positivism" title="Legal positivism">positivists</a> that a <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign state</a> could limit its authority to act by consenting to an agreement according to the contract principle <i><a href="/wiki/Pacta_sunt_servanda" title="Pacta sunt servanda">pacta sunt servanda</a></i>. This consensual view of international law was reflected in the 1920 Statute of the Permanent Court of International Justice, and remains preserved in Article 7 of the ICJ Statute.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sources_of_international_law" title="Sources of international law">sources of international law</a> applied by the community of nations are listed under Article 38 of the <a href="/wiki/Statute_of_the_International_Court_of_Justice" title="Statute of the International Court of Justice">Statute of the International Court of Justice</a>, which is considered authoritative in this regard:
</p>
<ol><li>International treaties and conventions;</li>
<li>International custom as derived from the "general practice" of states; and</li>
<li>General legal principles "recognized by civilized nations".</li></ol>
<p>Additionally, judicial decisions and the teachings of prominent international law scholars may be applied as "subsidiary means for the determination of rules of law".
</p><p>Many scholars agree that the fact that the sources are arranged sequentially suggests an implicit hierarchy of sources.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> However, the language of Article 38 does not explicitly hold such a hierarchy, and the decisions of the international courts and tribunals do not support such a strict hierarchy. By contrast, Article 21 of the <a href="/wiki/Rome_Statute_of_the_International_Criminal_Court" title="Rome Statute of the International Criminal Court">Rome Statute of the International Criminal Court</a> clearly defines a hierarchy of applicable law (or sources of international law).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Treaties">Treaties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Treaties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>International treaty law comprises obligations expressly and voluntarily accepted by states between themselves in <a href="/wiki/Treaty" title="Treaty">treaties</a>. Where there are disputes about the exact meaning and application of national laws, it is the responsibility of the courts to decide what the law means. In international law, interpretation is within the domain of the states concerned, but may also be conferred on judicial bodies such as the International Court of Justice, by the terms of the treaties or by consent of the parties. Thus, while it is generally the responsibility of states to interpret the law for themselves, the processes of diplomacy and availability of supra-national judicial organs routinely provide assistance to that end.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Convention_on_the_Law_of_Treaties" title="Vienna Convention on the Law of Treaties">Vienna Convention on the Law of Treaties</a>, which codifies several bedrock principles of treaty interpretation, holds that a treaty "shall be interpreted in <a href="/wiki/Good_faith" title="Good faith">good faith</a> in accordance with the ordinary meaning to be given to the terms of the treaty in their context and in the light of its object and purpose." This represents a compromise between three different theories of interpretation:
</p>
<ul><li>The <b>textual approach,</b> a restrictive interpretation that looks to the "ordinary meaning" of the text, assigning considerable weight to the actual text.</li>
<li>The <b>subjective approach</b>, which takes into consideration factors such as the ideas behind the treaty, the context of the treaty's creation, and what the drafters intended.</li>
<li>The <b>effective approach</b>, which interprets a treaty "in the light of its object and purpose", i.e. based on what best suits the goal of the treaty.</li></ul>
<p>The foregoing are general rules of interpretation, and do no preclude the application of specific rules for particular areas of international law.
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Greece_v_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Greece v United Kingdom">Greece v United Kingdom</a></i> [1952] <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.worldlii.org/int/cases/ICJ/1952/1.html">ICJ 1</a>, ICJ had no jurisdiction to hear a dispute between the UK government and a private Greek businessman under the terms of a treaty.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_v_Iran" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom v Iran">United Kingdom v Iran</a></i> [1952] <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.worldlii.org/int/cases/ICJ/1952/2.html">ICJ 2</a>, the ICJ did not have jurisdiction for a dispute over the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Iranian_Oil_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Iranian Oil Company">Anglo-Iranian Oil Company</a> being nationalised.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Oil_Platforms_case" title="Oil Platforms case">Oil Platforms case</a> (Islamic Republic of Iran v United States of America)</i> [2003] <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.worldlii.org/int/cases/ICJ/2003/4.html">ICJ 4</a>, rejected dispute over damage to ships which hit a mine.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_custom">International custom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: International custom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Customary international law is derived from the consistent practice of States accompanied by <i><a href="/wiki/Opinio_juris" class="mw-redirect" title="Opinio juris">opinio juris</a></i>, i.e. the conviction of states that the consistent practice is required by a legal obligation. Judgments of international tribunals as well as scholarly works have traditionally been looked to as persuasive sources for custom in addition to direct evidence of state behavior. Attempts to codify customary international law picked up momentum after the Second World War with the formation of the International Law Commission (ILC) under the aegis of the UN. Codified customary law is made the binding interpretation of the underlying custom by agreement through treaty. For states not party to such treaties, the work of the ILC may still be accepted as custom applying to those states. General principles of law are those commonly recognized by the major legal systems of the world. Certain norms of international law achieve the binding force of <a href="/wiki/Peremptory_norm" title="Peremptory norm">peremptory norms</a> (<i>jus cogens</i>) as to include all states with no permissible derogations.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Asylum_case" title="Asylum case">Colombia v Perú</a></i>  (1950), recognizing custom as a source of international law, but a practice of giving asylum was not part of it.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Belgium_v_Spain" class="mw-redirect" title="Belgium v Spain">Belgium v Spain</a></i> (1970), finding that only the state where a corporation is incorporated (not where its major shareholders reside) has standing to bring an action for damages for economic loss.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Statehood_and_responsibility">Statehood and responsibility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Statehood and responsibility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Monism_and_dualism_in_international_law" title="Monism and dualism in international law">Monism and dualism in international law</a></div>
<p>International law establishes the framework and the criteria for identifying <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">states</a> as the principal actors in the international legal system. As the existence of a state presupposes control and <a href="/wiki/Jurisdiction" title="Jurisdiction">jurisdiction</a> over territory, international law deals with the acquisition of territory, <a href="/wiki/State_immunity" title="State immunity">state immunity</a> and the legal responsibility of states in their conduct with each other. International law is similarly concerned with the treatment of individuals within state boundaries. There is thus a comprehensive regime dealing with group rights, the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Alien_(law)" title="Alien (law)">aliens</a>, the rights of <a href="/wiki/Refugee" title="Refugee">refugees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transnational_crime" title="Transnational crime">international crimes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nationality" title="Nationality">nationality</a> problems, and <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a> generally. It further includes the important functions of the maintenance of international peace and security, arms control, the pacific settlement of disputes and the regulation of the <a href="/wiki/Use_of_force" title="Use of force">use of force</a> in international relations. Even when the law is not able to stop the outbreak of war, it has developed principles to govern the conduct of hostilities and the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Prisoners_of_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Prisoners of war">prisoners</a>. International law is also used to govern issues relating to the global environment, the global commons such as <a href="/wiki/International_waters" title="International waters">international waters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">outer space</a>, global communications, and <a href="/wiki/International_trade" title="International trade">world trade</a>.
</p><p>In theory all states are <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereign</a> and equal. As a result of the notion of sovereignty, the value and authority of international law is dependent upon the voluntary participation of states in its formulation, observance, and enforcement. Although there may be exceptions, it is thought by many international academics that most states enter into legal commitments with other states out of <a href="/wiki/Enlightened_self-interest" title="Enlightened self-interest">enlightened self-interest</a> rather than adherence to a body of law that is higher than their own. As <a href="/w/index.php?title=D._W._Greig&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="D. W. Greig (page does not exist)">D. W. Greig</a> notes, "international law cannot exist in isolation from the political factors operating in the sphere of <a href="/wiki/International_relations" title="International relations">international relations</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditionally, <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign states</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See#Status_of_the_Holy_See_in_international_law" title="Holy See">Holy See</a> were the sole subjects of international law. With the proliferation of <a href="/wiki/International_organizations" class="mw-redirect" title="International organizations">international organizations</a> over the last century, they have in some cases been recognized as relevant parties as well. Recent interpretations of <a href="/wiki/International_human_rights_law" title="International human rights law">international human rights law</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_humanitarian_law" title="International humanitarian law">international humanitarian law</a>, and <a href="/wiki/International_trade_law" title="International trade law">international trade law</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/North_American_Free_Trade_Agreement" title="North American Free Trade Agreement">North American Free Trade Agreement</a> (NAFTA) Chapter 11 actions) have been inclusive of corporations, and even of certain individuals.
</p><p>The conflict between international law and national sovereignty is subject to vigorous debate and dispute in academia, diplomacy, and politics. Certainly, there is a growing trend toward judging a state's domestic actions in the light of international law and standards. Numerous people now view the nation-state as the primary unit of international affairs, and believe that only states may choose to voluntarily enter into commitments under international law, and that they have the right to follow their own counsel when it comes to interpretation of their commitments. Certain scholars<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (August 2012)">who?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and political leaders feel that these modern developments endanger nation states by taking power away from state governments and ceding it to international bodies such as the U.N. and the World Bank, argue that international law has evolved to a point where it exists separately from the mere consent of states, and discern a legislative and judicial process to international law that parallels such processes within domestic law.  This especially occurs when states violate or deviate from the expected standards of conduct adhered to by all civilized nations.
</p><p>A number of states place emphasis on the principle of territorial sovereignty, thus seeing states as having free rein over their internal affairs. Other states oppose this view. One group of opponents of this point of view, including many <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> nations, maintain that all civilized nations have certain norms of conduct expected of them, including the prohibition of <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Slave_trade" class="mw-redirect" title="Slave trade">slave trade</a>, <a href="/wiki/War_of_aggression" title="War of aggression">wars of aggression</a>, <a href="/wiki/Torture" title="Torture">torture</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">piracy</a>, and that violation of these universal norms represents a crime, not only against the individual victims, but against humanity as a whole. States and individuals who subscribe to this view opine that, in the case of the individual responsible for violation of international law, he "is become, like the <a href="/wiki/Piracy" title="Piracy">pirate</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Slave_trade" class="mw-redirect" title="Slave trade">slave trader</a> before him, <i><a href="/wiki/Hostis_humani_generis" title="Hostis humani generis">hostis humani generis</a></i>, an enemy of all mankind",<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> and thus subject to prosecution in a fair trial before any fundamentally just tribunal, through the exercise of <a href="/wiki/Universal_jurisdiction" title="Universal jurisdiction">universal jurisdiction</a>.
</p><p>Though the European democracies tend to support broad, universalistic interpretations of international law, many other democracies have differing views on international law. Several democracies, including <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, take a flexible, eclectic approach, recognizing aspects of international law such as territorial rights as universal, regarding other aspects as arising from treaty or custom, and viewing certain aspects as not being subjects of international law at all. Democracies in the developing world, due to their past colonial histories, often insist on non-interference in their internal affairs, particularly regarding human rights standards or their peculiar institutions, but often strongly support international law at the bilateral and multilateral levels, such as in the United Nations, and especially regarding the use of force, disarmament obligations, and the terms of the UN Charter.
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Case_Concerning_United_States_Diplomatic_and_Consular_Staff_in_Tehran" class="mw-redirect" title="Case Concerning United States Diplomatic and Consular Staff in Tehran">Case Concerning United States Diplomatic and Consular Staff in Tehran</a></i> [1980] <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.worldlii.org/int/cases/ICJ/1980/1.html">ICJ 1</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo_v_Belgium" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo v Belgium">Democratic Republic of the Congo v Belgium</a></i> [2002] <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.worldlii.org/int/cases/ICJ/2002/1.html">ICJ 1</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Territory_and_the_sea">Territory and the sea</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Territory and the sea">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Law_of_the_Sea" class="mw-redirect" title="Law of the Sea">Law of the Sea</a></div><p>The law of the sea is the area of international law concerning the principles and rules by which states and other entities interact in maritime matters.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> It encompasses areas and issues such as navigational rights, sea mineral rights, and coastal waters jurisdiction. The law of the sea is distinct from <b><a href="/wiki/Admiralty_law" title="Admiralty law">admiralty law</a></b> (also known as <b>maritime law</b>), which concerns relations and conduct at sea by private entities.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Convention_on_the_Law_of_the_Sea" title="United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea">United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea</a> (UNCLOS), concluded in 1982 and coming into force in 1994, is generally accepted as a codification of customary international law of the sea.
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Territorial_dispute" title="Territorial dispute">Territorial dispute</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Libya_v_Chad" class="mw-redirect" title="Libya v Chad">Libya v Chad</a></i> [1994] <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.worldlii.org/int/cases/ICJ/1994/1.html">ICJ 1</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_v_Norway" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom v Norway">United Kingdom v Norway</a></i> [1951] <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.worldlii.org/int/cases/ICJ/1951/3.html">ICJ 3</a>, the Fisheries case, concerning the limits of Norway's jurisdiction over neighboring waters</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Peru_v_Chile" class="mw-redirect" title="Peru v Chile">Peru v Chile</a></i> (2014) dispute over international waters.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Bakassi" title="Bakassi">Bakassi</a> case</i> [2002] <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.worldlii.org/int/cases/ICJ/2002/2.html">ICJ 2</a>, between Nigeria and Cameroon</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso-Niger_frontier_dispute_case" class="mw-redirect" title="Burkina Faso-Niger frontier dispute case">Burkina Faso-Niger frontier dispute case</a></i> (2013)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Convention_on_the_Law_of_the_Sea" title="United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea">United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Corfu_Channel_Case" class="mw-redirect" title="Corfu Channel Case">Corfu Channel Case</a></i> [1949] <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.worldlii.org/int/cases/ICJ/1949/1.html">ICJ 1</a>, UK sues Albania for damage to ships in international waters. First ICJ decision.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/France_v_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="France v United Kingdom">France v United Kingdom</a></i> [1953] <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.worldlii.org/int/cases/ICJ/1953/3.html">ICJ 3</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Germany_v_Denmark_and_the_Netherlands" class="mw-redirect" title="Germany v Denmark and the Netherlands">Germany v Denmark and the Netherlands</a></i> [1969] <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.worldlii.org/int/cases/ICJ/1969/1.html">ICJ 1</a>, successful claim for a greater share of the North Sea continental shelf by Germany. The ICJ held that the matter ought to be settled, not according to strict legal rules, but through applying equitable principles.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Case_concerning_maritime_delimitation_in_the_Black_Sea_(Romania_v_Ukraine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Case concerning maritime delimitation in the Black Sea (Romania v Ukraine)">Case concerning maritime delimitation in the Black Sea (Romania v Ukraine)</a></i> [2009] <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.worldlii.org/int/cases/ICJ/2009/3.html">ICJ 3</a></li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_and_economic_policy">Social and economic policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Social and economic policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Labor_law">Labor law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Labor law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="navigation" class="navbox" aria-labelledby="International_labour_sources" style="width: 350px; text-align: center; font-size: 80%; line-height: 1.5em; background-color: #fafafa; float: <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/International_criminal_law" title="International criminal law">International criminal law</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Court" title="International Criminal Court">International Criminal Court</a></div>
