<table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2012</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Courts_and_enforcement">Courts and enforcement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Courts and enforcement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>It is probably the case that almost all nations observe almost all principles of international law and almost all of their obligations almost all the time.</p><div class="templatequotecite">—&#8201;<cite><a href="/wiki/Louis_Henkin" title="Louis Henkin">Louis Henkin</a><sup id="cite_ref-Henkin_Quote_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Henkin_Quote-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>Since international law has no established compulsory <a href="/wiki/Judicial_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Judicial system">judicial system</a> for the settlement of disputes or a coercive <a href="/wiki/Penal_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Penal system">penal system</a>, it is not as straightforward as managing breaches within a domestic legal system. However, there are means by which breaches are brought to the attention of the international community and some means for resolution. For example, there are judicial or quasi-judicial tribunals in international law in certain areas such as trade and human rights. The formation of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, for example, created a means for the world community to enforce international law upon members that violate its charter through the Security Council.
</p><p>Since international law exists in a legal environment without an overarching "sovereign" (i.e., an external power able and willing to compel compliance with international norms), "enforcement" of international law is very different from in the domestic context. In many cases, enforcement takes on <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Coase" title="Ronald Coase">Coasian</a> characteristics, where the norm is self-enforcing.  In other cases, defection from the norm can pose a real risk, particularly if the international environment is changing.  When this happens, and if enough states (or enough powerful states) continually ignore a particular aspect of international law, the norm may actually change according to concepts of customary international law.  For example, prior to World War I, <a href="/wiki/Unrestricted_submarine_warfare" title="Unrestricted submarine warfare">unrestricted submarine warfare</a> was considered a violation of international law and ostensibly the <a href="/wiki/Casus_belli" title="Casus belli">casus belli</a> for the United States' declaration of war against Germany.  By World War II, however, the practice was so widespread that during the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_trials" title="Nuremberg trials">Nuremberg trials</a>, the charges against German Admiral <a href="/wiki/Karl_D%C3%B6nitz" title="Karl Dönitz">Karl Dönitz</a> for ordering unrestricted submarine warfare were dropped, notwithstanding that the activity constituted a clear violation of the Second London Naval Treaty of 1936.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_enforcement">Domestic enforcement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Domestic enforcement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Apart from a state's natural inclination to uphold certain norms, the force of international law comes from the pressure that states put upon one another to behave consistently and to honor their obligations. As with any system of law, many violations of international law obligations are overlooked. If addressed, it may be through <a href="/wiki/Diplomacy" title="Diplomacy">diplomacy</a> and the consequences upon an offending state's reputation, submission to international judicial determination,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> arbitration,<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> sanctions<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> or force including war.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Though violations may be common in fact, states try to avoid the appearance of having disregarded international obligations. States may also unilaterally adopt sanctions against one another such as the severance of economic or diplomatic ties, or through reciprocal action. In some cases, domestic courts may render judgment against a foreign state (the realm of private international law) for an injury, though this is a complicated area of law where international law intersects with domestic law.
</p><p>It is implicit in the Westphalian system of nation-states, and explicitly recognized under Article 51 of the <a href="/wiki/Charter_of_the_United_Nations" title="Charter of the United Nations">Charter of the United Nations</a>, that all states have the inherent right to individual and collective self-defense if an armed attack occurs against them. Article 51 of the UN Charter guarantees the right of states to defend themselves until (and unless) the Security Council takes measures to keep the peace.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_bodies">International bodies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: International bodies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/International_legal_system" title="International legal system">International legal system</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly_Resolution_377" title="United Nations General Assembly Resolution 377">United Nations General Assembly Resolution 377</a></div>
<p>As a "deliberative, policymaking and representative organ", the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> "is empowered to make recommendations"; it can neither codify international law nor make binding resolutions.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_GA-Powers_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_GA-Powers-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_Int-Law_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_Int-Law-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Merely internal resolutions, such as budgetary matters, may be binding on the operation of the General Assembly itself. Violations of the UN Charter by members of the United Nations may be raised by the aggrieved state in the General Assembly for debate.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly_resolution" title="United Nations General Assembly resolution">General Assembly resolutions</a> are generally non-binding towards member states, but through its adoption of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly_Resolution_377" title="United Nations General Assembly Resolution 377">"Uniting for Peace" resolution (A/RES/377 A)</a>, of 3 November 1950, the Assembly declared that it had the power to authorize the use of force, under the terms of the UN Charter, in cases of breaches of the peace or acts of aggression, provided that the Security Council, owing to the negative vote of a permanent member, fails to act to address the situation. The Assembly also declared, by its adoption of <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly_Resolution_377" title="United Nations General Assembly Resolution 377">resolution 377 A</a>, that it could call for other collective measures—such as economic and diplomatic sanctions—in situations constituting the milder "threat to the Peace".
</p><p>The Uniting for Peace resolution was initiated by the United States in 1950, shortly after the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, as a means of circumventing possible future Soviet vetoes in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">Security Council</a>. The legal role of the resolution is clear, given that the General Assembly can neither issue binding resolutions nor codify law. It was never argued by the "Joint Seven-Powers" that put forward the draft resolution,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> during the corresponding discussions, that it in any way afforded the Assembly new powers. Instead, they argued that the resolution simply declared what the Assembly's powers already were, according to the UN Charter, in the case of a dead-locked Security Council.<sup id="cite_ref-UN_APV299_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_APV299-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_APV300_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_APV300-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_APV301_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_APV301-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-UN_APV302_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_APV302-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The Soviet Union was the only permanent member of the Security Council to vote against the Charter interpretations that were made recommendation by the Assembly's adoption of resolution 377 A.
</p><p>Alleged violations of the Charter can also be raised by states in the Security Council. The Security Council could subsequently pass resolutions under Chapter VI of the UN Charter to recommend the "Pacific Resolution of Disputes." Such resolutions are not binding under international law, though they usually are expressive of the Council's convictions.  In rare cases, the Security Council can adopt resolutions under Chapter VII of the UN Charter, related to "threats to Peace, Breaches of the Peace and Acts of Aggression," which are legally binding under international law, and can be followed up with economic sanctions, military action, and similar uses of force through the auspices of the United Nations.
</p><p>It has been argued that resolutions passed outside of Chapter VII can also be binding; the legal basis for that is the Council's broad powers under Article 24(2), which states that "in discharging these duties (exercise of primary responsibility in international peace and security), it shall act in accordance with the Purposes and Principles of the United Nations". The mandatory nature of such resolutions was upheld by the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a>  (ICJ) in its advisory opinion on <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a>. The binding nature of such resolutions can be deduced from an interpretation of their language and intent.
</p><p>States can also, upon mutual consent, submit disputes for arbitration by the <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a>, located in <a href="/wiki/The_Hague" title="The Hague">The Hague</a>, <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>. The judgments given by the Court in these cases are binding, although it possesses no means to enforce its rulings.
The Court may give an advisory opinion on any legal question at the request of whatever body may be authorized by or in accordance with the Charter of the United Nations to make such a request. Some of the advisory cases brought before the court have been controversial with respect to the court's competence and jurisdiction.
</p><p>Often enormously complicated matters, ICJ cases (of which there have been less than 150 since the court was created from the <a href="/wiki/Permanent_Court_of_International_Justice" title="Permanent Court of International Justice">Permanent Court of International Justice</a> in 1945) can stretch on for years and generally involve thousands of pages of pleadings, evidence, and the world's leading specialist international lawyers. As of June 2009, there are 15 cases pending at the ICJ. Decisions made through other means of arbitration may be binding or non-binding depending on the nature of the arbitration agreement, whereas decisions resulting from contentious cases argued before the ICJ are always binding on the involved states.
</p><p>Though states (or increasingly, <a href="/wiki/International_organizations" class="mw-redirect" title="International organizations">international organizations</a>) are usually the only ones with standing to address a violation of international law, some treaties, such as the <a href="/wiki/International_Covenant_on_Civil_and_Political_Rights" title="International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights">International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights</a> have an optional <a href="/wiki/Protocol_(treaty)" class="mw-redirect" title="Protocol (treaty)">protocol</a> that allows individuals who have had their rights violated by member states to petition the international <a href="/wiki/Human_Rights_Committee" class="mw-redirect" title="Human Rights Committee">Human Rights Committee</a>. Investment treaties commonly and routinely provide for enforcement by individuals or investing entities.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> and commercial agreements of foreigners with sovereign governments may be enforced on the international plane.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_courts">International courts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: International courts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are numerous international bodies created by treaties adjudicating on legal issues where they may have jurisdiction. The only one claiming <a href="/wiki/Universal_jurisdiction" title="Universal jurisdiction">universal jurisdiction</a> is the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a>. Others are: the United Nations <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice" title="International Court of Justice">International Court of Justice</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Court" title="International Criminal Court">International Criminal Court</a> (when national systems have totally failed and the <a href="/wiki/Rome_Statute_of_the_International_Criminal_Court" title="Rome Statute of the International Criminal Court">Treaty of Rome</a> is applicable) and the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Arbitration_for_Sport" title="Court of Arbitration for Sport">Court of Arbitration for Sport</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="East_Africa_Community">East Africa Community</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: East Africa Community">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/East_African_Community" title="East African Community">East African Community</a></div>
<p>There were ambitions to make the East African Community, consisting of <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Burundi" title="Burundi">Burundi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rwanda" title="Rwanda">Rwanda</a>, a political federation with its own form of binding supranational law, but this effort has not materialized.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Union_of_South_American_Nations">Union of South American Nations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Union of South American Nations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Union_of_South_American_Nations" title="Union of South American Nations">Union of South American Nations</a></div>
<p>The Union of South American Nations serves the South American continent. It intends to establish a framework akin to the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> by the end of 2019. It is envisaged to have its own passport and currency, and limit barriers to trade.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Andean_Community_of_Nations">Andean Community of Nations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Andean Community of Nations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Andean_Community_of_Nations" class="mw-redirect" title="Andean Community of Nations">Andean Community of Nations</a></div>
<p>The Andean Community of Nations is the first attempt to integrate the countries of the <a href="/wiki/Andes_Mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Andes Mountains">Andes Mountains</a> in South America. It started with the Cartagena Agreement of 26 May 1969, and consists of four countries: <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colombia" title="Colombia">Colombia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecuador" title="Ecuador">Ecuador</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>. The Andean Community follows supranational laws, called Agreements, which are mandatory for these countries.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_legal_theory">International legal theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: International legal theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/International_legal_theories" title="International legal theories">International legal theories</a></div>
<p>International legal theory comprises a variety of theoretical and methodological approaches used to explain and analyse the content, formation and effectiveness of international law and institutions and to suggest improvements. Some approaches center on the question of compliance: why states follow international norms in the absence of a coercive power that ensures compliance. Other approaches focus on the problem of the formation of international rules: why states voluntarily adopt international law norms, that limit their freedom of action, in the absence of a world legislature; while other perspectives are policy oriented: they elaborate theoretical frameworks and instruments to criticize the existing norms and to make suggestions on how to improve them. Some of these approaches are based on domestic <a href="/wiki/Legal_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal theory">legal theory</a>, some are <a href="/wiki/Interdisciplinary" class="mw-redirect" title="Interdisciplinary">interdisciplinary</a>, and others have been developed expressly to analyse international law. Classical approaches to International legal theory are the <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">Natural law</a>, the Eclectic and the <a href="/wiki/Legal_positivism" title="Legal positivism">Legal positivism</a> schools of thought.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Natural_law" title="Natural law">natural law</a> approach argues that international norms should be based on <a href="/wiki/Axiomatic" class="mw-redirect" title="Axiomatic">axiomatic</a> truths. 16th-century natural law writer, <a href="/wiki/Francisco_de_Vitoria" title="Francisco de Vitoria">Francisco de Vitoria</a>, a professor of <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Salamanca" title="University of Salamanca">University of Salamanca</a>, examined the questions of the <a href="/wiki/Just_war" class="mw-redirect" title="Just war">just war</a>, the Spanish authority in the <a href="/wiki/Americas" title="Americas">Americas</a>, and the rights of the Native American peoples.
</p><p>In 1625 <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Grotius" title="Hugo Grotius">Hugo Grotius</a> argued that nations as well as persons ought to be governed by universal principle based on <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Divine_retribution" title="Divine retribution">divine justice</a> while the relations among polities ought to be governed by the law of peoples, the <i><a href="/wiki/Jus_gentium" title="Jus gentium">jus gentium</a></i>, established by the consent of the community of nations on the basis of the principle of <i><a href="/wiki/Pacta_sunt_servanda" title="Pacta sunt servanda">pacta sunt servanda</a></i>, that is, on the basis of the observance of commitments. On his part, <a href="/wiki/Emmerich_de_Vattel" class="mw-redirect" title="Emmerich de Vattel">Emmerich de Vattel</a> argued instead for the equality of states as articulated by 18th-century natural law and suggested that the law of nations was composed of custom and law on the one hand, and natural law on the other. During the 17th century, the basic tenets of the Grotian or <a href="/wiki/Eclecticism" title="Eclecticism">eclectic</a> school, especially the doctrines of legal equality, <a href="/wiki/Territorial_sovereignty" class="mw-redirect" title="Territorial sovereignty">territorial sovereignty</a>, and independence of states, became the fundamental principles of the European political and legal system and were enshrined in the 1648 <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Peace of Westphalia</a>.
</p><p>The early <a href="/wiki/Legal_positivism" title="Legal positivism">positivist</a> school emphasized the importance of custom and treaties as sources of international law. 16th-century <a href="/wiki/Alberico_Gentili" title="Alberico Gentili">Alberico Gentili</a> used historical examples to posit that positive law (<i>jus voluntarium</i>) was determined by general consent. <a href="/wiki/Cornelius_van_Bynkershoek" title="Cornelius van Bynkershoek">Cornelius van Bynkershoek</a> asserted that the bases of international law were customs and treaties commonly consented to by various states, while <a href="/w/index.php?title=John_Jacob_Moser&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="John Jacob Moser (page does not exist)">John Jacob Moser</a> emphasized the importance of state practice in international law. The positivism school narrowed the range of international practice that might qualify as law, favouring <a href="/wiki/Rationality" title="Rationality">rationality</a> over <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethic">ethics</a>. The 1815 <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Vienna" title="Congress of Vienna">Congress of Vienna</a> marked the formal recognition of the political and international legal system based on the conditions of Europe.
</p><p>Modern legal positivists consider international law as a unified system of rules that emanates from the states' will. International law, as it is, is an "<a href="/wiki/Objectivity_(philosophy)" title="Objectivity (philosophy)">objective</a>" reality that needs to be distinguished from law "as it should be." Classic positivism demands rigorous tests for <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">legal</a> <a href="/wiki/Validity_(logic)" title="Validity (logic)">validity</a> and it deems irrelevant all extralegal arguments.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticisms">Criticisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=International_law&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Criticisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Nation-states observe the principle of <i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Par_in_parem_non_habet_imperium" title="Par in parem non habet imperium">par in parem non habet imperium</a></i>, &#39;Between equals there is no sovereign power&#39;. <a href="/wiki/John_Austin_(legal_philosopher)" title="John Austin (legal philosopher)">John Austin</a> therefore asserted that 'so-called' international law, lacking a sovereign power and so unenforceable, was not really law at all, but 'positive morality', consisting of 'opinions and sentiments...more ethical than legal in nature.'<sup id="cite_ref-auto_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Article 2 (1) of the <a href="/wiki/UN_Charter" class="mw-redirect" title="UN Charter">UN Charter</a> confirms this Sovereignty of Nations; no state is in subjection to any other state.
</p><p>
Also, since the bulk of international law is treaty law, binding only on signatories, then; </p><blockquote><p> 'If legislation is the making of laws by a person or assembly binding on the whole community, there is no such thing as international law. For treaties bind only those who sign them.' </p></blockquote>
<p>Since states are few in number, diverse and atypical in character, unindictable, lacking a centralised sovereign power, and their agreements unpoliced and decentralised,<sup id="cite_ref-auto2_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> then, says Wight, 'international society is not a society at all. The condition of international relations is best described as international anarchy;
</p>
<blockquote><p> 'While in domestic politics the struggle for power is governed and circumscribed by law, in international politics, law is governed and circumscribed by the struggle for power. (This is why) international politics is called <a href="/wiki/Power_politics" title="Power politics">power politics</a>... War is the only means by which states can in the last resort defend vital interests...the causes of war are inherent in power politics.' </p></blockquote>
<p>On the subject of treaty law, <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a> said this; 'Treaties are like pretty girls, or roses; they last only as long as they last.'<sup id="cite_ref-auto3_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto3-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For <a href="/wiki/Hans_Morgenthau" title="Hans Morgenthau">Hans Morgenthau</a>, international law is the weakest and most primitive system of law enforcement. Its decentralised nature makes it similar to the law that prevails in preliterate tribal societies.<sup id="cite_ref-auto4_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto4-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Monopoly_on_violence" title="Monopoly on violence">monopoly on violence</a> is what makes domestic law enforceable; but between nations, there are multiple competing sources of force.<sup id="cite_ref-auto5_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto5-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The confusion created by treaty laws, which resemble private contracts between persons, is mitigated only by the relatively small number of states.<sup id="cite_ref-auto6_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto6-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> On the vital subject of war, it is unclear whether the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_trials" title="Nuremberg trials">Nuremberg trials</a> created new law, or applied the existing law of the <a href="/wiki/Kellogg-Briand_pact" class="mw-redirect" title="Kellogg-Briand pact">Kellogg-Briand pact</a>.
</p><p>Morgenthau asserts that no state may be compelled to submit a dispute to an international tribunal, making laws unenforceable and voluntary.<sup id="cite_ref-auto8_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto8-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> International law is also unpoliced, lacking agencies for enforcement.<sup id="cite_ref-auto9_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto9-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> He cites a 1947 US opinion poll in which 75% of respondents wanted 'an international police to maintain world peace'; but only 13% wanted that force to exceed the US armed forces. Later surveys have produced similar contradictory results.<sup id="cite_ref-auto10_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto10-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
